Painting Nodes Black With Red-Black Trees

Vaidehi Joshi
Aug 28, 2017= =C2=B7 16 min read
=

Well, we=E2=80=99d start by following the steps = we=E2=80=99re already familiar with: locate the position for the node and r= ecolor it to be red.

In our tree illustration, we=E2=80=99d be inserting our node <= code class=3D"kz lq lr ls lt b">5 as the right child of the black no= de 4. This wouldn=E2=80=99t violate= any rules, since neither of the two red nodes appear one after the other, = and all paths from the root node to null still pass through exactly 2 black nodes.

However, we could also recolor the = black parent node, 4, to be red instead of black. In this scenario= , we=E2=80=99re basically =E2=80=9Cpushing up=E2=80=9D the red from it=E2= =80=99s children, 3 and 5, so that their parent node becomes red. Noti= ce that we still don=E2=80=99t violate rules three or four, and we still pa= ss through exactly 2 black nodes in all the paths from the root to null.

As we=E2=80=99ve seen from this example, recoloring nodes is one = well-used technique for handling insertions (and deletions!) into a red-bla= ck tree.

The power of left and right rotations.

Another handy trick for = handling rule violations is one that we encountered earlier in this series:= rotations! We learned about rotations (or =E2=80=9Cglorified swaps=E2=80= =9D as I like to think of them) when we explored AVL trees.

We can do something similar when it comes to red-black= trees, too. In the drawing shown here, we have a red-black tree that is un= balanced; ignoring the colors of the nodes for a moment, let=E2=80=99s say = that we need to rotate and re-balance this tree.

In the first drawing, we are left-= rotating on the root node, 4, so th= at it=E2=80=99s right child, 11, be= comes the new parent. This is called a left-rotation. Similarly, if we right-rotate on the new = parent node, 11, and shift it down = so that it once again becomes the right child of 11, we=E2=80=99re performing a right-rotation.

Notice how the structure of the tree chang= es as we rotate in both of these cases; in our left-rotation, the subtree o= f 9-5-10 moved from the right subtr= ee to the left. Conversely, in our right-rotation, the same subtree of 9-5-10 moved from the left subtree back= to the right. Rotations tend to move around and restructure the subtrees of a larger red-black= tree, which can also be helpful in preventing any rule violations.

Let=E2=80=99s look = at one more example of how recoloring and rotation can help us in inserting= nodes into a red-black tree.

Remember: it=E2=80=99s easiest to start off by always= inserting a red node, and then recoloring and rotati= on as necessary, afterwards.

We=E2=80=99ll start with a single root node of 21, which will be red. But, since this is = the root node, and one of our rules is that the root node must always be bl= ack, we can recolor node 21 to be b= lack. Now, no rules are violated!

Next, we=E2=80=99ll insert a node with a value of= 8 into the tree, as the left child= of the root. We can insert it as a red node and not violate any rules in t= he process!

Next, we=E2=80=99ll insert a node with a value of 3. Inserting this node as the left child of 8 violates rule three, since we=E2=80=99ll ha= ve two consecutive red nodes. In order to fix this, we=E2=80=99ll need to r= otate the grandparent node (the root) and then recolor.

If we right-rotate the root node, and shift 21 down to become the right child of = 8, we=E2=80=99ve taken one step to = fixing our problem. The node 8 is o= ur new root node, with two children, 3 and 21.

However, now we=E2=80=99re viol= ating our root node rule yet again!

Not to worry =E2=80=94 we can just recolor our = original parent node of the newly-inserted node (which is now the right chi= ld), 21, and the root node, 8. If we recolor 8 and 21, our root n= ode is back to being black, and our two child nodes are both red.

And, most import= ant of all, no rules are violated, and we have a perfectly-balanced red-bla= ck tree!

= The benefits of painting it black

Okay, so we managed to recolor and rotate enough= in our first example to get things perfectly balanced and working correctl= y.

But, = what about inserting elements into a much larger red-black tree? Let=E2=80= =99s walk through a more complicated example; as we=E2=80=99ll see, the sam= e methods apply.

The key to = dealing with larger red-black trees is moving any rule violations up the tr= ee as we go.

In this example tree, we have= eight nodes, and we=E2=80=99re inserting node 10 into the tree. We=E2=80=99ll insert it as the left child of = 9, and color it red. Immediately, w= e can see that we=E2=80=99re violating rule three =E2=80=94 two red nodes a= re appearing consecutively.

We can first start off fixing this problem by recoloring the parent = nodes of our newly-inserted node, 10. This at least solves the problem of rule three being broken!

Well=E2=80=A6kind of. Actually, all we=E2=80=99ve done by recoloring her= e is shifted up our violation so that nodes 16 and 21 are now breaking rule three. We didn=E2=80=99t get rid of this violat= ion, we just moved it up!

We can=E2=80=99t recolor 16 to be black, because if we did that, we=E2=80=99d be violating r= ule four, and changing the number of black nodes that we=E2=80=99d pass thr= ough on all paths from the root node to nu= ll leaves. Since there are no parent nodes left to recolor (except f= or the root node, which we don=E2=80=99t want to make red!), we can now lea= n on rotations to help us out. Since node = 16 is violating the rule of consecutive reds, we can rotate this nod= e to the right.
